Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>\t\t\t\t<div id="contentSub"><span class="mw-redirectedfrom">\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0(Redirected from <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ornithologist&amp;redirect=no" class="mw-redirect" title="Ornithologist">Ornithologist</a>)</span></div>\n\t\t\t\t<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>\t\t\t\t<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>\n\t\t\t\t<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>\n\t\t\t\t<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">This article is about the field of zoology. For the jazz composition, see <a href="/wiki/Ornithology_(composition)" title="Ornithology (composition)">Ornithology (composition)</a>.</div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Banding_Marbled_Godwit_on_Bowdoin_NWR_(12820314955).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Banding_Marbled_Godwit_on_Bowdoin_NWR_%2812820314955%29.jpg/220px-Banding_Marbled_Godwit_on_Bowdoin_NWR_%2812820314955%29.jpg" width="220" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Banding_Marbled_Godwit_on_Bowdoin_NWR_%2812820314955%29.jpg/330px-Banding_Marbled_Godwit_on_Bowdoin_NWR_%2812820314955%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Banding_Marbled_Godwit_on_Bowdoin_NWR_%2812820314955%29.jpg/440px-Banding_Marbled_Godwit_on_Bowdoin_NWR_%2812820314955%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="743" data-file-height="498" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Banding_Marbled_Godwit_on_Bowdoin_NWR_(12820314955).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Marbled_godwit" title="Marbled godwit">marbled godwit</a> being <a href="/wiki/Bird_ringing" title="Bird ringing">ringed</a> for studies on <a href="/wiki/Bird_migration" title="Bird migration">bird migration</a></div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:LSU_Museum_of_Natural_Science_-_Study_skins_of_birds.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/LSU_Museum_of_Natural_Science_-_Study_skins_of_birds.jpg/220px-LSU_Museum_of_Natural_Science_-_Study_skins_of_birds.jpg" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/LSU_Museum_of_Natural_Science_-_Study_skins_of_birds.jpg/330px-LSU_Museum_of_Natural_Science_-_Study_skins_of_birds.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/LSU_Museum_of_Natural_Science_-_Study_skins_of_birds.jpg/440px-LSU_Museum_of_Natural_Science_-_Study_skins_of_birds.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2448" data-file-height="3264" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:LSU_Museum_of_Natural_Science_-_Study_skins_of_birds.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Bird_collections" title="Bird collections">collection</a> of bird skins</div></div></div>\n<p><b>Ornithology</b> is a branch of <a href="/wiki/Zoology" title="Zoology">zoology</a> that concerns the study of <a href="/wiki/Birds" class="mw-redirect" title="Birds">birds</a>. Several aspects of ornithology differ from related disciplines, due partly to the high visibility and the aesthetic appeal of birds.<sup id="cite_ref-newton_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-newton-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> Most marked among these is the extent of studies undertaken by amateurs working within the parameters of strict scientific methodology.\n</p><p>The science of ornithology has a long history and studies on birds have helped develop several key concepts in evolution, behaviour and ecology such as the definition of <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a>, the process of <a href="/wiki/Speciation" title="Speciation">speciation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Instinct" title="Instinct">instinct</a>, <a href="/wiki/Learning" title="Learning">learning</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ecological_niche" title="Ecological niche">ecological niches</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guild_(ecology)" title="Guild (ecology)">guilds</a>, <a href="/wiki/Island_biogeography" class="mw-redirect" title="Island biogeography">island biogeography</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phylogeography" title="Phylogeography">phylogeography</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bird_conservation" title="Bird conservation">conservation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-mayr_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mayr-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> While early ornithology was principally concerned with descriptions and distributions of species, ornithologists today seek answers to very specific questions, often using birds as models to test hypotheses or predictions based on theories. Most modern biological theories apply across taxonomic groups, and the number of professional scientists who identify themselves as "ornithologists" has therefore declined.<sup id="cite_ref-bibby2003_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bibby2003-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> A wide range of tools and techniques is used in ornithology, both inside the laboratory and out in the field, and innovations are constantly made.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a href="#Etymology"><span class="tocnumber">1</span> <span class="toctext">Etymology</span></a></li>\n<li <span class="toctext">Applications</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-12"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-13"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-14"><a href="#Additional_sources"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Additional sources</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-15"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ornithology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The word "ornithology" comes from the late 16th-century Latin <i>ornithologia</i> meaning "bird science" from the <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> \xe1\xbd\x84\xcf\x81\xce\xbd\xce\xb9\xcf\x82 <i>ornis</i> ("bird") and \xce\xbb\xcf\x8c\xce\xb3\xce\xbf\xcf\x82 <i>logos</i> ("theory, science, thought").<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ornithology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_ornithology" title="Timeline of ornithology">Timeline of ornithology</a></div>\n<p>The history of ornithology largely reflects the trends in the <a href="/wiki/History_of_biology" title="History of biology">history of biology</a>, as well as many other scientific disciplines, including <a href="/wiki/Ecology" title="Ecology">ecology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anatomy" title="Anatomy">anatomy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Physiology" title="Physiology">physiology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paleontology" title="Paleontology">paleontology</a>, and more recently, molecular biology. Trends include the move from mere descriptions to the identification of patterns, thus towards elucidating the processes that produce these patterns.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_knowledge_and_study">Early knowledge and study</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ornithology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Early knowledge and study">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Humans have had an observational relationship with birds since <a href="/wiki/Prehistory" title="Prehistory">prehistory</a>, with some stone-age drawings being amongst the oldest indications of an interest in birds.<sup id="cite_ref-Gurney,_JH_1921_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gurney,_JH_1921-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Birds were perhaps important as food sources, and bones of as many as 80 species have been found in excavations of early <a href="/wiki/Stone_Age" title="Stone Age">Stone Age</a> settlements.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Waterbird" class="mw-redirect" title="Waterbird">Waterbird</a> and <a href="/wiki/Seabird" title="Seabird">seabird</a> remains have also been found in <a href="/wiki/Shell_mound" class="mw-redirect" title="Shell mound">shell mounds</a> on the island of <a href="/wiki/Oronsay,_Inner_Hebrides" title="Oronsay, Inner Hebrides">Oronsay</a> off the coast of <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Gurney,_JH_1921_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gurney,_JH_1921-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:484px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Facsimile_Painting_of_Geese,_Tomb_of_Nefermaat_and_Itet_MET_DT226227.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Facsimile_Painting_of_Geese%2C_Tomb_of_Nefermaat_and_Itet_MET_DT226227.jpg/482px-Facsimile_Painting_of_Geese%2C_Tomb_of_Nefermaat_and_Itet_MET_DT226227.jpg" width="482" height="85" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Facsimile_Painting_of_Geese%2C_Tomb_of_Nefermaat_and_Itet_MET_DT226227.jpg/723px-Facsimile_Painting_of_Geese%2C_Tomb_of_Nefermaat_and_Itet_MET_DT226227.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Facsimile_Painting_of_Geese%2C_Tomb_of_Nefermaat_and_Itet_MET_DT226227.jpg/964px-Facsimile_Painting_of_Geese%2C_Tomb_of_Nefermaat_and_Itet_MET_DT226227.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3923" data-file-height="689" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Facsimile_Painting_of_Geese,_Tomb_of_Nefermaat_and_Itet_MET_DT226227.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Geese from a wall panel from the tomb of <a href="/wiki/Nefermaat" title="Nefermaat">Nefermaat</a>, Egypt c. 2575\xe2\x80\x932551 B.C.</div></div></div><p>\nCultures around the world have rich vocabularies related to birds.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Traditional bird names are often based on detailed knowledge of the behaviour, with many names being <a href="/wiki/Onomatopoeic" class="mw-redirect" title="Onomatopoeic">onomatopoeic</a>, and still in use.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Traditional knowledge may also involve the use of birds in folk medicine<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> and knowledge of these practices are passed on through oral traditions (see <a href="/wiki/Ethnoornithology" title="Ethnoornithology">ethno-ornithology</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> Hunting of wild birds as well as their domestication would have required considerable knowledge of their habits. <a href="/wiki/Poultry" title="Poultry">Poultry</a> farming and <a href="/wiki/Falconry" title="Falconry">falconry</a> were practised from early times in many parts of the world. Artificial incubation of poultry was practised in China around 246 BC and around at least 400 BC in Egypt.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> The Egyptians also made use of birds in their hieroglyphic scripts, many of which, though stylized, are still identifiable to species.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup></p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:BelonBirdSkel.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/BelonBirdSkel.jpg/220px-BelonBirdSkel.jpg" width="220" height="173" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/BelonBirdSkel.jpg/330px-BelonBirdSkel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/BelonBirdSkel.jpg/440px-BelonBirdSkel.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1164" data-file-height="914" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:BelonBirdSkel.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Belon\'s comparison of birds and humans in his <i>Book of Birds</i>, 1555</div></div></div><p>Early written records provide valuable information on the past distributions of species. For instance, <a href="/wiki/Xenophon" title="Xenophon">Xenophon</a> records the abundance of the <a href="/wiki/Ostrich" title="Ostrich">ostrich</a> in <a href="/wiki/Assyria" title="Assyria">Assyria</a> (Anabasis, i. 5); this subspecies from Asia Minor is extinct and all extant ostrich races are today restricted to <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a>. Other old writings such as the <a href="/wiki/Vedas" title="Vedas">Vedas</a> (1500\xe2\x80\x93800 BC) demonstrate the careful observation of avian life histories and include the earliest reference to the habit of <a href="/wiki/Brood_parasite" title="Brood parasite">brood parasitism</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Asian_koel" title="Asian koel">Asian koel</a> (<i>Eudynamys scolopacea</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> Like writing, the early art of China, Japan, Persia, and India also demonstrate knowledge, with examples of scientifically accurate bird illustrations.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> in 350 BC in his <i><a href="/wiki/History_of_animals" class="mw-redirect" title="History of animals">Historia Animalium</a></i><sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> noted the habit of <a href="/wiki/Bird_migration" title="Bird migration">bird migration</a>, moulting, egg laying, and lifespans, as well as compiling a list of 170 different bird species. However, he also introduced and propagated several myths, such as the idea that <a href="/wiki/Swallow" title="Swallow">swallows</a> <a href="/wiki/Hibernated" class="mw-redirect" title="Hibernated">hibernated</a> in winter, although he noted that <a href="/wiki/Common_crane" title="Common crane">cranes</a> migrated from the steppes of <a href="/wiki/Scythia" title="Scythia">Scythia</a> to the marshes at the headwaters of the <a href="/wiki/Nile" title="Nile">Nile</a>. The idea of swallow hibernation became so well established that even as late as in 1878, <a href="/wiki/Elliott_Coues" title="Elliott Coues">Elliott Coues</a> could list as many as 182 contemporary publications dealing with the hibernation of swallows and little published evidence to contradict the theory.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> Similar misconceptions existed regarding the breeding of barnacle geese. Their nests had not been seen, and  they were believed to grow by transformations of <a href="/wiki/Goose_barnacles" class="mw-redirect" title="Goose barnacles">goose barnacles</a>, an idea that became prevalent from around the 11th century and noted by Bishop Giraldus Cambrensis (<a href="/wiki/Gerald_of_Wales" title="Gerald of Wales">Gerald of Wales</a>) in <i><a href="/wiki/Topographia_Hiberniae" class="mw-redirect" title="Topographia Hiberniae">Topographia Hiberniae</a></i> (1187).<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> Around 77 AD, <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny the Elder</a> described birds, among other creatures, in his <i><a href="/wiki/Natural_History_(Pliny)" title="Natural History (Pliny)">Historia Naturalis</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The earliest record of falconry comes from the reign of Sargon II (722\xe2\x80\x93705 BC) in <a href="/wiki/Assyria" title="Assyria">Assyria</a>. Falconry is thought to have made its entry to Europe only after AD 400, brought in from the east after invasions by the <a href="/wiki/Huns" title="Huns">Huns</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alans" title="Alans">Alans</a>. Starting from the eighth century, numerous Arabic works on the subject and general ornithology were written, as well as translations of the works of ancient writers from Greek and <a href="/wiki/Syriac_language" title="Syriac language">Syriac</a>. In the 12th and 13th centuries,  crusades and conquest had subjugated Islamic territories in southern Italy, central Spain, and the Levant under European rule, and for the first time translations into Latin of the great works of Arabic and Greek scholars were made with the help of Jewish and Muslim scholars, especially in <a href="/wiki/Toledo,_Spain" title="Toledo, Spain">Toledo</a>, which had fallen into Christian hands in 1085 and whose libraries had escaped destruction. <a href="/wiki/Michael_Scot" title="Michael Scot">Michael Scotus</a> from Scotland made a Latin translation of Aristotle\'s work on animals from Arabic here around 1215, which was disseminated widely and was the first time in a millennium that this foundational text on zoology became available to Europeans. Falconry was popular in the <a href="/wiki/Normans" title="Normans">Norman</a> court in Sicily, and a number of works on the subject were written in <a href="/wiki/Palermo" title="Palermo">Palermo</a>. <a href="/wiki/Frederick_II,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Frederick II, Holy Roman Emperor">Emperor Frederick II of Hohenstaufen</a> (1194\xe2\x80\x931250) learned about an falconry during his youth in Sicily and later built up a <a href="/wiki/Menagerie" title="Menagerie">menagerie</a> and sponsored translations of Arabic texts, among which the popular Arabic work known as the <i><a href="/wiki/Moamyn" title="Moamyn">Liber Moaminus</a></i> by an unknown author which was translated into Latin by Theodore of Antioch from Syria in 1240-1241 as the <i>De Scientia Venandi per Aves</i>, and also Michael Scotus (who had removed to Palermo) translated <a href="/wiki/Avicenna" title="Avicenna">Ibn S\xc4\xabn\xc4\x81</a>\'s <i>Kit\xc4\x81b al-\xe1\xb8\xa4ayaw\xc4\x81n</i> of 1027 for the Emperor, a commentary and scientific update of Aristotle\'s work which was part of Ibn S\xc4\xabn\xc4\x81\'s massive <i><a href="/wiki/The_Book_of_Healing" title="The Book of Healing">Kit\xc4\x81b al-\xc5\xa0if\xc4\x81\xca\xbe</a></i>. Frederick II eventually wrote his own treatise on falconry, the <i><a href="/wiki/De_arte_venandi_cum_avibus" title="De arte venandi cum avibus">De arte venandi cum avibus</a></i>, in which he related his ornithological observations and the results of the hunts and experiments his court enjoyed performing.<sup id="cite_ref-egerton_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-egerton-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Several early German and French scholars compiled old works and conducted new research on birds. These included <a href="/wiki/Guillaume_Rondelet" title="Guillaume Rondelet">Guillaume Rondelet</a>, who described his observations in the Mediterranean, and <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Belon" title="Pierre Belon">Pierre Belon</a>, who described the fish and birds that he had seen in France and the Levant. Belon\'s <i>Book of Birds</i> (1555) is a folio volume with descriptions of some 200 species. His comparison of the skeleton of humans and birds is considered as a landmark in <a href="/wiki/Comparative_anatomy" title="Comparative anatomy">comparative anatomy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-miall_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-miall-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Volcher_Coiter" title="Volcher Coiter">Volcher Coiter</a> (1534\xe2\x80\x931576), a Dutch anatomist, made detailed studies of the internal structures of birds and produced a classification of birds, <i>De Differentiis Avium</i> (around 1572), that was based on structure and habits.<sup id="cite_ref-egallen_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-egallen-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Konrad_Gesner" class="mw-redirect" title="Konrad Gesner">Konrad Gesner</a> wrote the <i>Vogelbuch</i> and <i>Icones avium omnium</i> around 1557. Like Gesner, <a href="/wiki/Ulisse_Aldrovandi" title="Ulisse Aldrovandi">Ulisse Aldrovandi</a>, an encyclopedic naturalist, began a 14-volume natural history with three volumes on birds, entitled <i>ornithologiae hoc est de avibus historiae libri XII</i>, which was published from 1599 to 1603. Aldrovandi showed great interest in plants and animals, and his work included 3000 drawings of fruits, flowers, plants, and animals, published in 363 volumes. His <i>Ornithology</i> alone covers 2000 pages and included such aspects as the <a href="/wiki/Chicken" title="Chicken">chicken</a> and poultry techniques. He used a number of traits including behaviour, particularly bathing and dusting, to classify bird groups.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:AldrovandiOrnithology.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/AldrovandiOrnithology.jpg/170px-AldrovandiOrnithology.jpg" width="170" height="260" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/AldrovandiOrnithology.jpg/255px-AldrovandiOrnithology.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/AldrovandiOrnithology.jpg/340px-AldrovandiOrnithology.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="1223" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:AldrovandiOrnithology.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cover of <a href="/wiki/Ulisse_Aldrovandi" title="Ulisse Aldrovandi">Ulisse Aldrovandi</a>\'s <i>Ornithology</i>, 1599</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ValliDeTodi1601.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/ValliDeTodi1601.jpg/220px-ValliDeTodi1601.jpg" width="220" height="157" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/ValliDeTodi1601.jpg/330px-ValliDeTodi1601.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/ValliDeTodi1601.jpg/440px-ValliDeTodi1601.jpg 2x" data-file-width="708" data-file-height="506" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ValliDeTodi1601.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Antonio_Valli_da_Todi" title="Antonio Valli da Todi">Antonio Valli da Todi</a>, who wrote on <a href="/wiki/Aviculture" title="Aviculture">aviculture</a> in 1601, knew the connections between territory and song<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/William_Turner_(ornithologist)" class="mw-redirect" title="William Turner (ornithologist)">William Turner</a>\'s <i>Historia Avium</i> (<i>History of Birds</i>), published at <a href="/wiki/Cologne" title="Cologne">Cologne</a> in 1544, was an early ornithological work from England. He noted the commonness of <a href="/wiki/Red_kite" title="Red kite">kites</a> in English cities where they snatched food out of the hands of children. He included folk beliefs such as those of anglers. Anglers believed that the <a href="/wiki/Osprey" title="Osprey">osprey</a> emptied their fishponds and would kill them, mixing the flesh of the osprey into their fish bait. Turner\'s work reflected the violent times in which he lived, and stands in contrast to later works such as <a href="/wiki/Gilbert_White" title="Gilbert White">Gilbert White</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Natural_History_and_Antiquities_of_Selborne" title="The Natural History and Antiquities of Selborne">The Natural History and Antiquities of Selborne</a></i> that were written in a tranquil era.<sup id="cite_ref-miall_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-miall-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-gilbertwhite_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gilbertwhite-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the 17th century, <a href="/wiki/Francis_Willughby" title="Francis Willughby">Francis Willughby</a> (1635\xe2\x80\x931672) and <a href="/wiki/John_Ray" title="John Ray">John Ray</a> (1627\xe2\x80\x931705) came up with the first major system of bird classification that was based on function and morphology rather than on form or behaviour. Willughby\'s <i>Ornithologiae libri tres</i> (1676) completed by John Ray is sometimes considered to mark the beginning of scientific ornithology. Ray also worked on <i>Ornithologia</i>, which was published posthumously in 1713 as <i>Synopsis methodica avium et piscium</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-white_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-white-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> The earliest list of British birds, <i>Pinax Rerum Naturalium Britannicarum</i>, was written by <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Merrett" class="mw-redirect" title="Christopher Merrett">Christopher Merrett</a> in 1667, but authors such as John Ray considered it of little value.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Ray did, however, value the expertise of the naturalist <a href="/wiki/Sir_Thomas_Browne" class="mw-redirect" title="Sir Thomas Browne">Sir Thomas Browne</a> (1605\xe2\x80\x9382), who not only answered his queries on ornithological identification and nomenclature, but also those of Willoughby and Merrett in letter correspondence. Browne himself in his lifetime kept an eagle, owl, cormorant, bittern, and ostrich, penned a tract on falconry, and introduced the words "incubation" and "oviparous" into the English language.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> \n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:An_Experiment_on_a_Bird_in_an_Air_Pump_by_Joseph_Wright_of_Derby,_1768.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/An_Experiment_on_a_Bird_in_an_Air_Pump_by_Joseph_Wright_of_Derby%2C_1768.jpg/220px-An_Experiment_on_a_Bird_in_an_Air_Pump_by_Joseph_Wright_of_Derby%2C_1768.jpg" width="220" height="162" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/An_Experiment_on_a_Bird_in_an_Air_Pump_by_Joseph_Wright_of_Derby%2C_1768.jpg/330px-An_Experiment_on_a_Bird_in_an_Air_Pump_by_Joseph_Wright_of_Derby%2C_1768.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/An_Experiment_on_a_Bird_in_an_Air_Pump_by_Joseph_Wright_of_Derby%2C_1768.jpg/440px-An_Experiment_on_a_Bird_in_an_Air_Pump_by_Joseph_Wright_of_Derby%2C_1768.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5639" data-file-height="4160" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:An_Experiment_on_a_Bird_in_an_Air_Pump_by_Joseph_Wright_of_Derby,_1768.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/An_Experiment_on_a_Bird_in_the_Air_Pump" title="An Experiment on a Bird in the Air Pump">An Experiment on a Bird in the Air Pump</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Wright_of_Derby" title="Joseph Wright of Derby">Joseph Wright of Derby</a>, 1768</div></div></div>\n<p>Towards the late 18th century, <a href="/wiki/Mathurin_Jacques_Brisson" title="Mathurin Jacques Brisson">Mathurin Jacques Brisson</a> (1723\xe2\x80\x931806) and <a href="/wiki/Georges-Louis_Leclerc,_Comte_de_Buffon" title="Georges-Louis Leclerc, Comte de Buffon">Comte de Buffon</a> (1707\xe2\x80\x931788) began new works on birds. Brisson produced a six-volume work <i>Ornithologie</i> in 1760 and Buffon\'s included nine volumes (volumes 16\xe2\x80\x9324) on birds <i>Histoire naturelle des oiseaux</i> (1770\xe2\x80\x931785) in his work on science <i>Histoire naturelle g\xc3\xa9n\xc3\xa9rale et particuli\xc3\xa8re</i> (1749\xe2\x80\x931804). Jacob Temminck sponsored <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Le_Vaillant" class="mw-redirect" title="Fran\xc3\xa7ois Le Vaillant">Fran\xc3\xa7ois Le Vaillant</a> [1753\xe2\x80\x931824] to collect bird specimens in Southern Africa and Le Vaillant\'s six-volume <i>Histoire naturelle des oiseaux d\'Afrique</i> (1796\xe2\x80\x931808) included many non-African birds. His other bird books produced in collaboration with the artist Barraband are considered among the most valuable illustrated guides ever produced. <a href="/wiki/Louis_Jean_Pierre_Vieillot" title="Louis Jean Pierre Vieillot">Louis Jean Pierre Vieillot</a> (1748\xe2\x80\x931831) spent 10 years studying North American birds and wrote the <i>Histoire naturelle des oiseaux de l\'Amerique septentrionale</i> (1807\xe2\x80\x931808?). Vieillot pioneered in the use of life histories and habits in classification.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> Alexander Wilson composed a nine-volume work, <i>American Ornithology</i>, published 1808-14\xe2\x80\x94the first such record of North American birds, significantly antedating Audubon. In the early 19th century, <a href="/wiki/Lewis_and_Clark" class="mw-redirect" title="Lewis and Clark">Lewis and Clark</a> studied and identified many birds in the western United States. <a href="/wiki/John_James_Audubon" title="John James Audubon">John James Audubon</a>, born in 1785, observed and painted birds in France and later in the <a href="/wiki/Ohio" title="Ohio">Ohio</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mississippi" title="Mississippi">Mississippi</a> valleys. From 1827 to 1838, Audubon published <i><a href="/wiki/Birds_of_America_(book)" class="mw-redirect" title="Birds of America (book)">The Birds of America</a></i>, which was engraved by <a href="/wiki/Havell_family" title="Havell family">Robert Havell, Sr.</a> and his son Robert Havell, Jr. Containing 435 engravings, it is often regarded as the greatest ornithological work in history.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Scientific_studies">Scientific studies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ornithology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Scientific studies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bird_eggs.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Bird_eggs.jpg/220px-Bird_eggs.jpg" width="220" height="280" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Bird_eggs.jpg/330px-Bird_eggs.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Bird_eggs.jpg/440px-Bird_eggs.jpg 2x" data-file-width="598" data-file-height="760" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bird_eggs.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Early bird study focused on collectibles such as eggs and nests.</div></div></div>\n<p>The emergence of ornithology as a scientific discipline began in the 18th century, when <a href="/wiki/Mark_Catesby" title="Mark Catesby">Mark Catesby</a> published his two-volume <i>Natural History of Carolina, Florida, and the Bahama Islands</i>, a landmark work which included 220 hand-painted engravings and was the basis for many of the species <a href="/wiki/Carl_Linnaeus" title="Carl Linnaeus">Carl Linnaeus</a> described in the 1758 <i><a href="/wiki/10th_edition_of_Systema_Naturae" title="10th edition of Systema Naturae">Systema Naturae</a></i>. Linnaeus\' work revolutionised bird taxonomy by assigning every species a <a href="/wiki/Binomial_nomenclature" title="Binomial nomenclature">binomial name</a>, categorising them into different genera.\nHowever, ornithology did not emerge as a specialised science until the Victorian era\xe2\x80\x94with the concept of natural history, and the collection of natural objects such as bird eggs and skins.<sup id="cite_ref-allen_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-allen-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> This specialization led to the formation in Britain of the <a href="/wiki/British_Ornithologists%27_Union" title="British Ornithologists&#39; Union">British Ornithologists\' Union</a> in 1858. In 1859, the members founded its journal <i><a href="/wiki/The_Ibis" class="mw-redirect" title="The Ibis">The Ibis</a></i>. The sudden spurt in ornithology was also due in part to <a href="/wiki/Colonialism" title="Colonialism">colonialism</a>. At 100 years later, in 1959, <a href="/wiki/Reginald_Ernest_Moreau" title="Reginald Ernest Moreau">R. E. Moreau</a> noted that ornithology in this period was preoccupied with the geographical distributions of various species of birds.<sup id="cite_ref-ibis-history_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ibis-history-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>No doubt the preoccupation with widely extended geographical ornithology, was fostered by the immensity of the areas over which British rule or influence stretched during the 19th century and for some time afterwards.</p><div class="templatequotecite">\xe2\x80\x94&#8201;<cite>Moreau<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup></cite></div>\n</blockquote>\n<p>The bird collectors of the Victorian era observed the variations in bird forms and habits across geographic regions, noting local specialization and variation in widespread species. The collections of museums and private collectors grew with contributions from various parts of the world. The naming of species with binomials and the organization of birds into groups based on their similarities became the main work of museum specialists. The variations in widespread birds across geographical regions caused the introduction of trinomial names.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:KaupClassification.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/KaupClassification.svg/220px-KaupClassification.svg.png" width="220" height="183" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/KaupClassification.svg/330px-KaupClassification.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/KaupClassification.svg/440px-KaupClassification.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1860" data-file-height="1548" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:KaupClassification.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Kaup\'s classification of the crow family</div></div></div>\n<p>The search for patterns in the variations of birds was attempted by many. <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Wilhelm_Joseph_Schelling" title="Friedrich Wilhelm Joseph Schelling">Friedrich Wilhelm Joseph Schelling</a> (1775\xe2\x80\x931854), his student <a href="/wiki/Johann_Baptist_von_Spix" title="Johann Baptist von Spix">Johann Baptist von Spix</a> (1781\xe2\x80\x931826), and several others believed that a hidden and innate mathematical order existed in the forms of birds. They believed that a "natural" classification was available and superior to "artificial" ones. A particularly popular idea was the <a href="/wiki/Quinarian_system" title="Quinarian system">Quinarian system</a> popularised by <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_Aylward_Vigors" title="Nicholas Aylward Vigors">Nicholas Aylward Vigors</a> (1785\xe2\x80\x931840), <a href="/wiki/William_Sharp_Macleay" title="William Sharp Macleay">William Sharp Macleay</a> (1792\xe2\x80\x931865), <a href="/wiki/William_John_Swainson" title="William John Swainson">William Swainson</a>, and others. The idea was that nature followed a "rule of five" with five groups nested hierarchically. Some had attempted a rule of four, but <a href="/wiki/Johann_Jakob_Kaup" title="Johann Jakob Kaup">Johann Jakob Kaup</a> (1803\xe2\x80\x931873) insisted that the number five was special, noting that other natural entities such as the senses also came in fives. He followed this idea and demonstrated his view of the order within the crow family. Where he failed to find five genera, he left a blank insisting that a new genus would be found to fill these gaps. These ideas were replaced by more complex "maps" of affinities in works by <a href="/wiki/Hugh_Edwin_Strickland" title="Hugh Edwin Strickland">Hugh Edwin Strickland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Russel_Wallace" title="Alfred Russel Wallace">Alfred Russel Wallace</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ohara_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ohara-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> A major advance was made by <a href="/wiki/Max_F%C3%BCrbringer" title="Max F\xc3\xbcrbringer">Max F\xc3\xbcrbringer</a> in 1888, who established a comprehensive phylogeny of birds based on anatomy, morphology, distribution, and biology. This was developed further by <a href="/wiki/Hans_Gadow" class="mw-redirect" title="Hans Gadow">Hans Gadow</a> and others.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Galapagos" class="mw-redirect" title="Galapagos">Galapagos</a> <a href="/wiki/Finch" title="Finch">finches</a> were especially influential in the development of <a href="/wiki/Charles_Darwin" title="Charles Darwin">Charles Darwin</a>\'s theory of evolution. His contemporary <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Russel_Wallace" title="Alfred Russel Wallace">Alfred Russel Wallace</a> also noted these variations and the geographical separations between different forms leading to the study of <a href="/wiki/Biogeography" title="Biogeography">biogeography</a>. Wallace was influenced by the work of <a href="/wiki/Philip_Lutley_Sclater" class="mw-redirect" title="Philip Lutley Sclater">Philip Lutley Sclater</a> on the distribution patterns of birds.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:SwainsonQuinarian.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/SwainsonQuinarian.jpg/220px-SwainsonQuinarian.jpg" width="220" height="225" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/SwainsonQuinarian.jpg/330px-SwainsonQuinarian.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/SwainsonQuinarian.jpg/440px-SwainsonQuinarian.jpg 2x" data-file-width="667" data-file-height="683" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:SwainsonQuinarian.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Quinarian_system" title="Quinarian system">Quinarian system</a> of bird classification by Swainson</div></div></div>\n<p>For Darwin, the problem was how species arose from a common ancestor, but he did not attempt to find rules for delineation of species. The <a href="/wiki/Species_problem" title="Species problem">species problem</a> was tackled by the ornithologist <a href="/wiki/Ernst_Mayr" title="Ernst Mayr">Ernst Mayr</a>, who was able to demonstrate that geographical isolation and the accumulation of genetic differences led to the splitting of species.<sup id="cite_ref-konishi_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-konishi-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Early ornithologists were preoccupied with matters of species identification. Only systematics counted as true science and field studies were considered inferior through much of the 19th century.<sup id="cite_ref-haffer_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-haffer-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> In 1901, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Ridgway" title="Robert Ridgway">Robert Ridgway</a> wrote in the introduction to <i>The Birds of North and Middle America</i> that:\n</p>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>There are two essentially different kinds of ornithology: systematic or scientific, and popular. The former deals with the structure and classification of birds, their synonymies, and technical descriptions. The latter treats of their habits, songs, nesting, and other facts pertaining to their life histories.\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>This early idea that the study of living birds was merely recreation held sway until ecological theories became the predominant focus of ornithological studies.<sup id="cite_ref-mayr_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mayr-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ibis-history_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ibis-history-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> The study of birds in their habitats was particularly advanced in Germany with <a href="/wiki/Bird_ringing" title="Bird ringing">bird ringing</a> stations established as early as 1903. By the 1920s, the <i>Journal f\xc3\xbcr Ornithologie</i> included many papers on the behaviour, ecology, anatomy, and physiology, many written by <a href="/wiki/Erwin_Stresemann" title="Erwin Stresemann">Erwin Stresemann</a>. Stresemann changed the editorial policy of the journal, leading both to a unification of field and laboratory studies and a shift of research from museums to universities.<sup id="cite_ref-haffer_50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-haffer-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> Ornithology in the United States continued to be dominated by museum studies of morphological variations, species identities, and geographic distributions, until it was influenced by Stresemann\'s student Ernst Mayr.<sup id="cite_ref-barrow_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-barrow-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> In Britain, some of the earliest ornithological works that used the word ecology appeared in 1915.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> <i>The Ibis</i>, however, resisted the introduction of these new methods of study, and  no paper on ecology appeared until 1943.<sup id="cite_ref-ibis-history_41-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ibis-history-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> The work of <a href="/wiki/David_Lack" title="David Lack">David Lack</a> on population ecology was pioneering. Newer quantitative approaches were introduced for the study of ecology and behaviour, and this was not readily accepted. For instance, <a href="/wiki/Claud_Ticehurst" class="mw-redirect" title="Claud Ticehurst">Claud Ticehurst</a> wrote:\n</p>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Sometimes it seems that elaborate plans and statistics are made to prove what is commonplace knowledge to the mere collector, such as that hunting parties often travel more or less in circles.</p><div class="templatequotecite">\xe2\x80\x94&#8201;<cite>Ticehurst<sup id="cite_ref-ibis-history_41-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ibis-history-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup></cite></div>\n</blockquote>\n<p>David Lack\'s studies on population ecology sought to find the processes involved in the regulation of population based on the evolution of optimal clutch sizes. He concluded that population was regulated primarily by <a href="/wiki/Density-dependent_inhibition" class="mw-redirect" title="Density-dependent inhibition">density-dependent controls</a>, and also suggested that natural selection produces life-history traits that maximize the fitness of individuals. Others, such as <a href="/wiki/V._C._Wynne-Edwards" title="V. C. Wynne-Edwards">Wynne-Edwards</a>, interpreted population regulation as a mechanism that aided the <a href="/wiki/Group_selection" title="Group selection">"species" rather than individuals</a>. This led to widespread and sometimes bitter debate on what constituted the "unit of selection".<sup id="cite_ref-konishi_48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-konishi-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> Lack also pioneered the use of many new tools for ornithological research, including the idea of using <a href="/wiki/Radar_ornithology" title="Radar ornithology">radar</a> to study bird migration.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Birds were also widely used in studies of the niche hypothesis and <a href="/wiki/Georgii_Gause" class="mw-redirect" title="Georgii Gause">Georgii Gause</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Competitive_exclusion" class="mw-redirect" title="Competitive exclusion">competitive exclusion</a> principle. Work on resource partitioning and the structuring of bird communities through competition were made by <a href="/wiki/Robert_MacArthur" class="mw-redirect" title="Robert MacArthur">Robert MacArthur</a>. Patterns of <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity" title="Biodiversity">biodiversity</a> also became a topic of interest. Work on the relationship of the number of species to area and its application in the study of <a href="/wiki/Island_biogeography" class="mw-redirect" title="Island biogeography">island biogeography</a> was pioneered by <a href="/wiki/E._O._Wilson" title="E. O. Wilson">E. O. Wilson</a> and <a href="/wiki/Robert_MacArthur" class="mw-redirect" title="Robert MacArthur">Robert MacArthur</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-konishi_48-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-konishi-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> These studies led to the development of the discipline of <a href="/wiki/Landscape_ecology" title="Landscape ecology">landscape ecology</a>.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Red-footed_Falcon.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Red-footed_Falcon.jpeg/220px-Red-footed_Falcon.jpeg" width="220" height="338" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Red-footed_Falcon.jpeg/330px-Red-footed_Falcon.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/77/Red-footed_Falcon.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="391" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Red-footed_Falcon.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A mounted specimen of a <a href="/wiki/Red-footed_falcon" title="Red-footed falcon">red-footed falcon</a></div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/John_Crook_(ethologist)" title="John Crook (ethologist)">John Hurrell Crook</a> studied the behaviour of <a href="/wiki/Weaverbird" class="mw-redirect" title="Weaverbird">weaverbirds</a> and demonstrated the links between ecological conditions, behaviour, and social systems.<sup id="cite_ref-konishi_48-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-konishi-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Crook1980_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Crook1980-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> Principles from economics were introduced to the study of biology by Jerram L. Brown in his work on explaining territorial behaviour. This led to more studies of behaviour that made use of cost-benefit analyses.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> The rising interest in <a href="/wiki/Sociobiology" title="Sociobiology">sociobiology</a> also led to a spurt of bird studies in this area.<sup id="cite_ref-konishi_48-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-konishi-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The study of imprinting behaviour in ducks and geese by <a href="/wiki/Konrad_Lorenz" title="Konrad Lorenz">Konrad Lorenz</a> and the studies of instinct in <a href="/wiki/European_herring_gull" title="European herring gull">herring gulls</a> by <a href="/wiki/Nicolaas_Tinbergen" class="mw-redirect" title="Nicolaas Tinbergen">Nicolaas Tinbergen</a> led to the establishment of the field of <a href="/wiki/Ethology" title="Ethology">ethology</a>. The study of learning became an area of interest and the study of <a href="/wiki/Birdsong" class="mw-redirect" title="Birdsong">bird songs</a> has been a model for studies in neuroethology. The study of hormones and physiology in the control of behaviour has also been aided by bird models. These have helped in finding the <a href="/wiki/Proximate_and_ultimate_causation" title="Proximate and ultimate causation">proximate causes</a> of circadian and seasonal cycles. Studies on migration have attempted to answer questions on the evolution of migration, orientation, and navigation.<sup id="cite_ref-konishi_48-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-konishi-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The growth of genetics and the rise of molecular biology led to the application of the <a href="/wiki/Gene-centered_view_of_evolution" title="Gene-centered view of evolution">gene-centered view of evolution</a> to explain avian phenomena. Studies on kinship and altruism, such as <a href="/wiki/Helpers_at_the_nest" title="Helpers at the nest">helpers</a>, became of particular interest. The idea of <a href="/wiki/Inclusive_fitness" title="Inclusive fitness">inclusive fitness</a> was used to interpret observations on behaviour and life history, and birds were widely used models for testing hypotheses based on theories postulated by <a href="/wiki/W._D._Hamilton" title="W. D. Hamilton">W. D. Hamilton</a> and others.<sup id="cite_ref-konishi_48-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-konishi-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The new tools of molecular biology changed the study of bird systematics, which changed from being based on <a href="/wiki/Phenotype" title="Phenotype">phenotype</a> to the underlying <a href="/wiki/Genotype" title="Genotype">genotype</a>. The use of techniques such as <a href="/wiki/DNA-DNA_hybridization" class="mw-redirect" title="DNA-DNA hybridization">DNA-DNA hybridization</a> to study evolutionary relationships was pioneered by <a href="/wiki/Charles_Sibley" title="Charles Sibley">Charles Sibley</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jon_Edward_Ahlquist" class="mw-redirect" title="Jon Edward Ahlquist">Jon Edward Ahlquist</a>, resulting in what is called the <a href="/wiki/Sibley-Ahlquist_taxonomy" class="mw-redirect" title="Sibley-Ahlquist taxonomy">Sibley-Ahlquist taxonomy</a>. These early techniques have been replaced by newer ones based on <a href="/wiki/Mitochondrial_DNA" title="Mitochondrial DNA">mitochondrial DNA</a> sequences and <a href="/wiki/Molecular_phylogenetics" title="Molecular phylogenetics">molecular phylogenetics</a> approaches that make use of computational procedures for <a href="/wiki/Sequence_alignment" title="Sequence alignment">sequence alignment</a>, construction of <a href="/wiki/Phylogenetic_tree" title="Phylogenetic tree">phylogenetic trees</a>, and calibration of <a href="/wiki/Molecular_clock" title="Molecular clock">molecular clocks</a> to infer evolutionary relationships.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> Molecular techniques are also widely used in studies of avian <a href="/wiki/Population_biology" title="Population biology">population biology</a> and ecology.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rise_to_popularity">Rise to popularity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ornithology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Rise to popularity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The use of <a href="/wiki/Field_glass" class="mw-redirect" title="Field glass">field glasses</a> or <a href="/wiki/Telescope" title="Telescope">telescopes</a> for bird observation began in the 1820s and 1830s, with pioneers such as <a href="/wiki/John_Freeman_Milward_Dovaston" title="John Freeman Milward Dovaston">J. Dovaston</a> (who also pioneered in the use of bird feeders), but instruction manuals did not begin to insist on the use of optical aids such as "a first-class telescope" or "field glass" until the 1880s.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:VillageBirdsFlorenceMerriam.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/VillageBirdsFlorenceMerriam.jpg/220px-VillageBirdsFlorenceMerriam.jpg" width="220" height="332" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/VillageBirdsFlorenceMerriam.jpg/330px-VillageBirdsFlorenceMerriam.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/VillageBirdsFlorenceMerriam.jpg/440px-VillageBirdsFlorenceMerriam.jpg 2x" data-file-width="728" data-file-height="1098" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:VillageBirdsFlorenceMerriam.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Page from an early field guide by <a href="/wiki/Florence_Augusta_Merriam_Bailey" title="Florence Augusta Merriam Bailey">Florence Augusta Merriam Bailey</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The rise of field guides for the identification of birds was another major innovation. The early guides such as those of <a href="/wiki/History_of_British_Birds" class="mw-redirect" title="History of British Birds">Thomas Bewick (two volumes)</a> and <a href="/wiki/History_of_British_Birds_(1843)" class="mw-redirect" title="History of British Birds (1843)">William Yarrell (three volumes)</a> were cumbersome, and mainly focused on identifying specimens in the hand. The earliest of the new generation of field guides was prepared by <a href="/wiki/Florence_Augusta_Merriam_Bailey" title="Florence Augusta Merriam Bailey">Florence Merriam</a>, sister of <a href="/wiki/Clinton_Hart_Merriam" title="Clinton Hart Merriam">Clinton Hart Merriam</a>, the mammalogist. This was published in 1887 in a series <i>Hints to Audubon Workers:Fifty Birds and How to Know Them</i> in Grinnell\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Audubon_Magazine" class="mw-redirect" title="Audubon Magazine">Audubon Magazine</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-barrow_51-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-barrow-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> These were followed by new field guides including classics by <a href="/wiki/Roger_Tory_Peterson" title="Roger Tory Peterson">Roger Tory Peterson</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The interest in <a href="/wiki/Birdwatching" title="Birdwatching">birdwatching</a> grew in popularity in many parts of the world, and the possibility for amateurs to contribute to biological studies was soon realized. As early as 1916, <a href="/wiki/Julian_Huxley" title="Julian Huxley">Julian Huxley</a> wrote a two-part article in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Auk" title="The Auk">The Auk</a></i>, noting the tensions between amateurs and professionals, and suggested the possibility that the "vast army of bird lovers and bird watchers could begin providing the data scientists needed to address the fundamental problems of biology."<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Organizations were started in many countries, and these grew rapidly in membership, most notable among them being the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society_for_the_Protection_of_Birds" title="Royal Society for the Protection of Birds">Royal Society for the Protection of Birds</a> (RSPB) in Britain and the <a href="/wiki/Audubon_Society" class="mw-redirect" title="Audubon Society">Audubon Society</a> in the US, which started in 1885. Both these organizations were started with the primary objective of conservation. The RSPB, born in 1889, grew from a small group of women in <a href="/wiki/Croydon" title="Croydon">Croydon</a>, who met regularly and called themselves the "Fur, Fin, and Feather Folk" and who took a pledge "to refrain from wearing the feathers of any birds not killed for the purpose of food, the ostrich only exempted." The organization did not allow men as members initially, avenging a policy of the British Ornithologists\' Union to keep out women.<sup id="cite_ref-allen_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-allen-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> Unlike the RSPB, which was primarily conservation oriented, the British Trust for Ornithology was started in 1933 with the aim of advancing ornithological research. Members were often involved in collaborative ornithological projects. These projects have resulted in atlases which detail the distribution of bird species across Britain.<sup id="cite_ref-bibby2003_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bibby2003-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> In the United States, the Breeding Bird Surveys, conducted by the US Geological Survey, have also produced atlases with information on breeding densities and changes in the density and distribution over time. Other volunteer collaborative ornithology projects were subsequently established in other parts of the world.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Techniques">Techniques</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ornithology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Techniques">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The tools and techniques of ornithology are varied, and new inventions and approaches are quickly incorporated. The techniques may be broadly dealt under the categories of those that are applicable to specimens and those that are used in the field, but the classification is rough and many analysis techniques are usable both in the laboratory and field or may require a combination of field and laboratory techniques.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Collections">Collections</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ornithology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Collections">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Bird_collections" title="Bird collections">Bird collections</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Godwit_prep.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Godwit_prep.jpg/220px-Godwit_prep.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Godwit_prep.jpg/330px-Godwit_prep.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Godwit_prep.jpg/440px-Godwit_prep.jpg 2x" data-file-width="720" data-file-height="540" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Godwit_prep.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bird-preservation techniques</div></div></div>\n<p>The earliest approaches to modern bird study involved the collection of eggs, a practice known as <a href="/wiki/Oology" title="Oology">oology</a>. While collecting became a pastime for many amateurs, the labels associated with these early egg collections made them unreliable for the serious study of bird breeding. To preserve eggs, a tiny hole was made and the contents extracted. This technique became standard with the invention of the blow drill around 1830.<sup id="cite_ref-allen_39-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-allen-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> Egg collection is no longer popular; however, historic museum collections have been of value in determining the effects of <a href="/wiki/Pesticide" title="Pesticide">pesticides</a> such as <a href="/wiki/DDT" title="DDT">DDT</a> on physiology.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> Museum <a href="/wiki/Bird_collections" title="Bird collections">bird collections</a> continue to act as a resource for taxonomic studies.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:BirdMorpho.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/BirdMorpho.svg/220px-BirdMorpho.svg.png" width="220" height="98" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/BirdMorpho.svg/330px-BirdMorpho.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/BirdMorpho.svg/440px-BirdMorpho.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="337" data-file-height="150" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:BirdMorpho.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Morphometric measurements of birds are important in systematics.</div></div></div>\n<p>The use of bird skins to document species has been a standard part of systematic ornithology. Bird skins are prepared by retaining the key bones of the wings, legs, and skull along with the skin and feathers. In the past, they were treated with <a href="/wiki/Arsenic" title="Arsenic">arsenic</a> to prevent fungal and insect (mostly <a href="/wiki/Dermestidae" title="Dermestidae">dermestid</a>) attack. Arsenic, being toxic, was replaced by less-toxic <a href="/wiki/Borax" title="Borax">borax</a>. Amateur and professional collectors became familiar with these skinning techniques and started sending in their skins to museums, some of them from distant locations. This led to the formation of huge collections of bird skins in museums in Europe and North America. Many private collections were also formed. These became references for comparison of species, and the ornithologists at these museums were able to compare species from different locations, often places that they themselves never visited. <a href="/wiki/Morphometrics" title="Morphometrics">Morphometrics</a> of these skins, particularly the lengths of the tarsus, bill, tail, and wing became important in the descriptions of bird species. These skin collections have been used in more recent times for studies on <a href="/wiki/Molecular_phylogenetics" title="Molecular phylogenetics">molecular phylogenetics</a> by the extraction of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_DNA" title="Ancient DNA">ancient DNA</a>. The importance of <a href="/wiki/Biological_type" class="mw-redirect" title="Biological type">type specimens</a> in the description of species make skin collections a vital resource for systematic ornithology. However, with the rise of molecular techniques,  establishing the taxonomic status of new discoveries, such as the <a href="/wiki/Bulo_Burti_boubou" class="mw-redirect" title="Bulo Burti boubou">Bulo Burti boubou</a> (<i>Laniarius liberatus</i>, no longer a valid species) and the <a href="/wiki/Bugun_liocichla" title="Bugun liocichla">Bugun liocichla</a> (<i>Liocichla bugunorum</i>), using blood, DNA and feather samples as the <a href="/wiki/Holotype" title="Holotype">holotype</a> material, has now become possible.\n</p><p>Other methods of preservation include the storage of specimens in spirit. Such wet specimens have special value in physiological and anatomical study, apart from providing better quality of DNA for molecular studies.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Freeze_drying" class="mw-redirect" title="Freeze drying">Freeze drying</a> of specimens is another technique that has the advantage of preserving stomach contents and anatomy, although it tends to shrink, making it less reliable for morphometrics.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_the_field">In the field</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ornithology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: In the field">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The study of birds in the field was helped enormously by improvements in optics. Photography made it possible to document birds in the field with great accuracy. High-power spotting scopes today allow observers to detect minute morphological differences that were earlier possible only by examination of the specimen "in the hand".<sup id="cite_ref-shorebirds_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shorebirds-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mist_net_kinglet.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Mist_net_kinglet.jpg/220px-Mist_net_kinglet.jpg" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Mist_net_kinglet.jpg/330px-Mist_net_kinglet.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Mist_net_kinglet.jpg/440px-Mist_net_kinglet.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1704" data-file-height="2272" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mist_net_kinglet.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A bird caught in a <a href="/wiki/Mist_net" title="Mist net">mist net</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The capture and marking of birds enable detailed studies of life history. Techniques for capturing birds are varied and include the use of <a href="/wiki/Birdlime" title="Birdlime">bird liming</a> for perching birds, <a href="/wiki/Mist_net" title="Mist net">mist nets</a> for woodland birds, <a href="/wiki/Cannon_netting" class="mw-redirect" title="Cannon netting">cannon netting</a> for open-area flocking birds, the <i><a href="/wiki/Bal-chatri" title="Bal-chatri">bal-chatri</a></i> trap for raptors,<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> decoys and <a href="/wiki/Heligoland_trap" title="Heligoland trap">funnel traps</a> for water birds.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Monitoreo_de_Aves_a_Largo_Plazo.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Monitoreo_de_Aves_a_Largo_Plazo.jpg/220px-Monitoreo_de_Aves_a_Largo_Plazo.jpg" width="220" height="330" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Monitoreo_de_Aves_a_Largo_Plazo.jpg/330px-Monitoreo_de_Aves_a_Largo_Plazo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Monitoreo_de_Aves_a_Largo_Plazo.jpg/440px-Monitoreo_de_Aves_a_Largo_Plazo.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1035" data-file-height="1553" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Monitoreo_de_Aves_a_Largo_Plazo.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A researcher measures a wild woodpecker.  The bird\'s right leg has a metal identification tag.</div></div></div><p>\nThe bird in the hand may be examined and <a href="/wiki/Morphometrics" title="Morphometrics">measurements</a> can be made, including standard lengths and weights. Feather moult and skull ossification provide indications of age and health. Sex can be determined by examination of anatomy in some sexually nondimorphic species. Blood samples may be drawn to determine hormonal conditions in studies of physiology, identify DNA markers for studying genetics and kinship in studies of breeding biology and phylogeography. Blood may also be used to identify pathogens and <a href="/wiki/Arthropod-borne_virus" class="mw-redirect" title="Arthropod-borne virus">arthropod-borne viruses</a>. <a href="/wiki/Ectoparasite" class="mw-redirect" title="Ectoparasite">Ectoparasites</a> may be collected for studies of coevolution and <a href="/wiki/Zoonoses" class="mw-redirect" title="Zoonoses">zoonoses</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> In many cryptic species, measurements (such as the relative lengths of wing feathers in warblers) are vital in establishing identity.</p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Condor_in_flight.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Condor_in_flight.JPG/220px-Condor_in_flight.JPG" width="220" height="330" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Condor_in_flight.JPG/330px-Condor_in_flight.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Condor_in_flight.JPG/440px-Condor_in_flight.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1414" data-file-height="2121" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Condor_in_flight.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/California_condor" title="California condor">California condor</a> marked with wing tags</div></div></div><p>Captured birds are often marked for future recognition. <a href="/wiki/Bird_ringing" title="Bird ringing">Rings or bands</a> provide long-lasting identification, but require capture for the information on them to be read. Field-identifiable marks such as coloured bands, wing tags, or dyes enable short-term studies where individual identification is required. <a href="/wiki/Mark_and_recapture" title="Mark and recapture">Mark and recapture</a> techniques make <a href="/wiki/Demographic" class="mw-redirect" title="Demographic">demographic</a> studies possible. Ringing has traditionally been used in the study of migration. In recent times, satellite transmitters provide the ability to track migrating birds in near-real time.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Techniques for estimating <a href="/wiki/Population_density" title="Population density">population density</a> include <a href="/wiki/Avian_ecology_field_methods#Point_counts_and_area_searches" title="Avian ecology field methods">point counts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Transect" title="Transect">transects</a>, and territory mapping. Observations are made in the field using carefully designed protocols and the data may be analysed to estimate bird diversity, relative abundance, or absolute population densities.<sup id="cite_ref-bibby_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bibby-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> These methods may be used repeatedly over large timespans to monitor changes in the environment.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Camera_trap" title="Camera trap">Camera traps</a> have been found to be a useful tool for the detection and documentation of elusive species, nest predators and in the quantitative analysis of frugivory, seed dispersal and behaviour.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_the_laboratory">In the laboratory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ornithology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: In the laboratory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Many aspects of bird biology are difficult to study in the field. These include the study of behavioural and physiological changes that require a long duration of access to the bird. Nondestructive samples of blood or feathers taken during field studies may be studied in the laboratory. For instance, the variation in the ratios of stable hydrogen isotopes across latitudes makes establishing the origins of migrant birds possible using <a href="/wiki/Mass_spectrometry" title="Mass spectrometry">mass spectrometric</a> analysis of feather samples.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> These techniques can be used in combination with other techniques such as ringing.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The first attenuated vaccine developed by <a href="/wiki/Louis_Pasteur" title="Louis Pasteur">Louis Pasteur</a>, for fowl cholera, was tested on poultry in 1878.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> Anti-malarials were tested on birds which harbour avian-malarias.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> Poultry continues to be used as a model for many studies in non-mammalian immunology.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>\nStudies in bird behaviour include the use of tamed and trained birds in captivity. Studies on <a href="/wiki/Bird_intelligence" title="Bird intelligence">bird intelligence</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bird_vocalization#Learning" title="Bird vocalization">song learning</a> have been largely laboratory-based. Field researchers may make use of a wide range of techniques such as the use of dummy owls to elicit mobbing behaviour, and dummy males or the use of call playback to elicit territorial behaviour and thereby to establish the boundaries of bird territories.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup></p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:EmlenFunnel.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/EmlenFunnel.svg/220px-EmlenFunnel.svg.png" width="220" height="109" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/EmlenFunnel.svg/330px-EmlenFunnel.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/EmlenFunnel.svg/440px-EmlenFunnel.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="868" data-file-height="432" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:EmlenFunnel.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An Emlen funnel is used to study the orientation behaviour of migratory birds in a laboratory. Experimenters sometimes place the funnel inside a planetarium to study night migration.</div></div></div><p>Studies of <a href="/wiki/Bird_migration" title="Bird migration">bird migration</a> including aspects of navigation, orientation, and physiology are often studied using captive birds in special cages that record their activities. The <a href="/wiki/Emlen_funnel" title="Emlen funnel">Emlen funnel</a>, for instance, makes use of a cage with an inkpad at the centre and a conical floor where the ink marks can be counted to identify the direction in which the bird attempts to fly. The funnel can have a transparent top and visible cues such as the direction of sunlight may be controlled using mirrors or the positions of the stars simulated in a <a href="/wiki/Planetarium" title="Planetarium">planetarium</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The entire genome of the domestic fowl (<i><a href="/wiki/Gallus_gallus" class="mw-redirect" title="Gallus gallus">Gallus gallus</a></i>) was sequenced in 2004, and was followed in 2008 by the genome of the zebra finch (<i><a href="/wiki/Taeniopygia_guttata" class="mw-redirect" title="Taeniopygia guttata">Taeniopygia guttata</a></i>).<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>  Such whole-genome sequencing projects allow for studies on evolutionary processes involved in <a href="/wiki/Speciation" title="Speciation">speciation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> Associations between the expression of genes and behaviour may be studied using candidate genes. Variations in the exploratory behaviour of great tits (<i><a href="/wiki/Parus_major" class="mw-redirect" title="Parus major">Parus major</a></i>) have been found to be linked with a gene orthologous to the human gene <i><a href="/wiki/DRD4" class="mw-redirect" title="DRD4">DRD4</a></i> (Dopamine receptor D4) which is known to be associated with novelty-seeking behaviour.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> The role of gene expression in developmental differences and morphological variations have been studied in <a href="/wiki/Darwin%27s_finches" title="Darwin&#39;s finches">Darwin\'s finches</a>. The difference in the expression of <i>Bmp4</i> have been shown to be associated with changes in the growth and shape of the beak.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The chicken has long been a <a href="/wiki/Model_organism" title="Model organism">model organism</a> for studying vertebrate <a href="/wiki/Developmental_biology" title="Developmental biology">developmental biology</a>. As the embryo is readily accessible, its development can be easily followed (unlike <a href="/wiki/House_mouse" title="House mouse">mice</a>).
